Pledgor's Right to Revoke Before Possession
States the pledgor's right to revoke before possession with qiyas to gift.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the pledgor's right to revoke a pledge before possession and apply the qiyas (analogy) between pledge and gift to explain the legal basis.

Core Idea
A pledge is revocable before possession because the pledgor is a benefactor, just like in a gift.

In Hanafi fiqh, the pledge (rahn) contract is not immediately binding. This lesson examines the key principle that the pledgor, as a benefactor (mutabarriʻ), may withdraw from the contract at any time before the pledgee takes physical possession. The ruling is derived by analogy to gifts and charity, which are also gratuitous contracts.

Guided Path
1
State the Ruling
Start by identifying the precise ruling from the matn: the pledgor may revoke (yarjiʻ) the pledge as long as the pledgee has not taken possession (qabd).
2
Identify the Illa (Effective Cause)
The reason given in the commentary is that the pledgor is a mutabarriʻ (benefactor) – he receives no direct countervalue. In Hanafi law, gratuitous contracts are not binding until delivery is complete.
Mutabarriʻ is a key term: one who gives without receiving compensation.
3
Trace the Qiyas (Analogy)
Recognize that the pledge is being analogized to a gift (hiba). Both are tabarruʻāt (gratuitous acts). In a gift, the donor may revoke before possession; likewise in a pledge.
The analogy is jaliy (clear) because the illa is evident: absence of countervalue.
4
Note the Cross-Madhhab Difference
Imam Malik held that the pledge is binding from the moment of contract, arguing it is a security akin to a sale. The Hanafi school responds that the pledge remains gratuitous and therefore revocable.
This ikhtilaf has practical consequences: in Maliki fiqh, the pledgor cannot unilaterally withdraw.
5
Confirm the Mufta Bihi
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school is the permission to revoke before possession, as stated in Zahir al-Riwayah.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa today.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وله أن يرجع إلخ: يعني يجوز للراهن أن يرجع عن الرهن ما لم يقبضه المرتهن؛ لما ذكرنا أنه متبرع، ولا لزوم على المتبرع ما لم يسلم بالكلية كالهبة والصدقة، وفيه خلاف مالك، وقد ذكرناه [ رمز الحقائق: ٤٠٤/٢]
And he may revoke, etc.: meaning it is permissible for the pledgor to revoke the pledge as long as the pledgee has not taken possession of it, because he is a benefactor (mutabarriʻ), and a benefactor is not bound until he has fully delivered, like a gift or charity. Imam Malik differed on this, and we have mentioned it earlier.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage explains the matn phrase 'and he may revoke the pledge' (wa lahu an yarjiʻa ʻan al-rahn). The commentator clarifies that the pledgor has the right to cancel the pledge contract before the pledgee acquires possession (qabd). The reason is that the pledgor is considered a benefactor (mutabarriʻ) – he is not receiving any direct benefit from the pledge – and a benefactor is not legally bound until he has completely transferred the item. This is analogous to a gift (hiba) or charity (sadaqa), where the donor may withdraw before the recipient takes possession. The passage notes that Imam Malik disagreed, holding that the contract is binding from the outset, but the Hanafi view is relied upon.

Key Terms
متبرع (mutabarriʻ): A benefactor; one who gives something without receiving a countervalue. The pledgor is considered a benefactor because he grants the pledge as security without direct compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man pledges his watch to a friend as collateral for a loan, but before handing it over, he decides not to. According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling?
A) He must hand it over because the contract is binding.
B) He may revoke because he is a benefactor and the pledgee has not yet possessed it.
C) He must pay damages for revoking.
D) The contract is void ab initio.

Key Takeaway
A pledge is revocable before possession because the pledgor is a benefactor, and gratuitous contracts are not binding until delivery.

Liability of the Pledgee: Guarantee for the Lesser of Value and Debt
Establishes pledgee liability with proof and scenarios

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the pledgee's liability after possession and apply it to three scenarios of destruction.

Core Idea
The pledgee is liable for the lesser of the pledge's value and the debt, not the full value.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq establishes a key Hanafi principle: once the pledgee takes possession, the pledge enters his guarantee. But the guarantee is limited. Understanding this limitation and its proof is essential for correctly applying the rulings of rahn. We'll explore the Hanafi position, the evidence, and the differing views of al-Shafi'i and Malik.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
The passage begins by stating that upon possession (qabd), the pledge becomes 'madhmun' (guaranteed). This means the pledgee is liable if it is destroyed, but only up to a certain limit.
The guarantee does not make the pledgee owner; it is a form of daman.
2
Understand the limitation: the lesser of value and debt
The text clarifies that the guarantee is for 'al-aqall min qimatihi wa min al-dayn' – the lesser of the pledge's value and the debt. This is the core Hanafi position.
If the value exceeds the debt, the debt is fulfilled; if the value is less, only that amount is deducted.
3
Examine the evidence
The Hanafi proof is the hadith of the man who pledged a horse and it died; the Prophet (peace be upon him) said 'Your right is gone', indicating the debt is considered paid to the extent of the pledge's value. Also consensus of the Companions.
The opposing schools use different hadith; the Hanafi response interprets them differently.
4
Note the valuation and three scenarios
The value is assessed at the time of possession. Three cases: (1) value equals debt – debt is fully discharged; (2) value exceeds debt – debt discharged, surplus is trust; (3) value less than debt – debt reduced by value, pledgor owes balance.
This is the practical application of the ruling.
5
Compare with other schools
Al-Shafi'i considers the entire pledge a trust with no liability; Malik distinguishes between apparent and hidden causes of destruction. The Hanafi school follows the stronger evidence and practice of the Companions.
The thamarah al-khilaf: debt reduction vs full debt remaining.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو مضمون إلخ : يعني إذا قبض المرتمن الرهن دخل في ضمانه، فإذا هلك المرهون في يده بعد قبضه يضمن الأقل من قيمة الرهن ومن الدين هذا عندنا، وقال الشافعي الله الرهن كله أمانة في يد المرتمن لا يسقط من الدين شيء بهلاكه؛ لقوله عليه: "لا يغلق الرهن من صاحبه الذي ،رهنه له غنمه وعليه غرمه، وقال مالك: إن هلك بأمر ظاهر، فهو في ضمان الراهن، وإن هلك بأمر خفي، فهو في ضمان المرتمن. ولنا: ما روي أن رجلا رهن فرسا، فنفق فاختصما إلى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فقال: "ذهب حقك"، وفي رواية: "الرهن بما فيه"، وإجماع الصحابة على أن الرهن مضمون والتفصيل مذكور في "العيني" وغيره فليراجع. وقوله: "مضمون" أن ماليته ،مضمونة وأما عينه فأمانة، قال في "الاختيار " : ويهلك على ملك الراهن؛ لأنه ملكه حقيقة، وهو أمانة في يد المرتهن حتى لو اشتراه لا ينوب قبض الرهن عن قبض الشراء؛ لأنه أمانة فلا ينوب عن قبض الضمان وإذا كان ملكه فمات كان كفنه عليه عيني، فتح بزيادة من المحشي) يعني بأقل من قيمته ومن الدين إلخ وتعتبر القيمة يوم القبض؛ لأنه يومئذ دخل في ضمانه، وإن اختلفا في القيمة، فالقول للمرتهن؛ لأنه ينكر الزيادة، والبينة للراهن؛ لأنه يثبتها، كذا في "الفتح" عن "الاختيار"، ولو قال: هو مضمون بالأقل، لكان أحسن وأظهر؛ لأنه مع التعريف لا يتوهم كون "من" تفضيلية، بخلاف التنكير، وكونها تفضيلية مشكل؛ لأنه إن أريد أنه مضمون بأقل من المجموع، أو بأقل من أحدهما بأن كان الواو بمعنى "أو"، فهذا شيء غير مفيد، بل المراد أنه مضمون بما هو أقل من الآخر. (كذا في الفتح) فلو هلك إلخ: هذا تفريع على كون الرهن مضمونا بأقل من قيمته ومن الرهن وبيانه: أنه لو هلك الرهن عند المرتمن، فإن كانت قيمة الرهن مثل الدين صار المرتمن مستوفيا دينه حكما، وإن كانت قيمته أكثر من دينه، صار المرتمن بقدر دينه مستوفيا والفضل أمانة في يد المرتهن يضمنه إن كان متعديا وإن كان الهلاك من غير تعد فلا ضمان عليه، وإن كانت قيمته أقل من الدين صار المرتمن مستوفيا بقدر الرهن ورجع على الراهن بفضل الدين، مثلا: إذا كان الدين مائة درهم والرهن أيضا يساوي مائة درهم، فهلك من غير تعد، صار المرتمن مستوفيا دينه حكما، ولا يبقى له مطالبة بالرهن وإن كان الرهن يساوي مائة وخمسين درهما مثلا، فالخمسون أمانة في يده، فلا يضمنها إلا بالتعدي وإن كان الرهن يساوي تسعين يصير المرتمن مستوفيا من دينه تسعين ويرجع على الراهن بعشرة دراهم رمز الحقائق: ٤٠٤/٢] وله أن يطالب الراهن إلخ : أي للمرتهن أن يطالب الراهن بدينه، ويحبسه به، وإن كان الرهن في يده؛ لأن حقه باق، والرهن لزيادة الصيانة، فلا تمتنع المطالبة، وكذا لا يمتنع الحبس به؛ لأنه جزاء الظلم، وهو المماطلة على ما بيناه في القضاء مفصلا تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٧٨/٨] درهما، ويؤمر المرتهن أي إذا طلب المرتمن ،دينه يؤمر بإحضار الرهن أولا؛ ليعلم أنه باق، ولأنه قبض الرهن قبل الاستيفاء، ولا يجوز أن يقبض ماله مع قيام يد الاستيفاء؛ لأنه يؤدي إلى تكرار الاستيفاء على اعتبار الهلاك في يد المرتهن، وهو يحتمل، ولو قال: بإحضار رهنه لو في يده لكان أولى؛ ليخرج ما إذا كان في يد عدل، فإنه لا يؤمر بإحضاره كما سنبين، وإذا أحضر المرتمن الرهن أمر الراهن بتسليم الدين أولا، وهو المراد بقوله: "والراهن بأداء دينه أولا"؛ ليتعين حق المرتهن في الدين كما تعين حق الراهن في حق الرهن تحقيقا للتسوية بينهما كما في تسليم المبيع والثمن. وإن طالبه بالدين في غير البلد الذي وقع العقد فيه، فإن كان الرهن لا حمل له ولا مؤونة، فكذلك الجواب؛ لأن الأماكن كلها في حقه كبقعة واحدة في حق التسليم، وإن كان له حمل ومؤونة، فيستوفي دينه، ولا يكلف إحضار -
And it is guaranteed, etc.: meaning when the pledgee takes possession of the pledge, it enters his guarantee. So if the pledged item is destroyed in his hand after possession, he is liable for the lesser of the value of the pledge and the debt. This is our view. Al-Shafi’i said: the entire pledge is a trust in the hand of the pledgee, nothing of the debt is diminished by its destruction, due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'The pledge is not closed from the one who pledged it; he has its gain and he bears its loss.' Malik said: if it is destroyed by an apparent cause, it is in the guarantee of the pledgor; if by a hidden cause, it is in the guarantee of the pledgee. Our proof: it is narrated that a man pledged a horse, and it died; they disputed before the Prophet (peace be upon him) and he said: 'Your right is gone.' In another narration: 'The pledge covers its value.' And there is consensus of the Companions that the pledge is guaranteed... The value is considered on the day of possession, because on that day it entered his guarantee... If the pledge perishes: if the value of the pledge equals the debt, the pledgee becomes as if he has received his debt; if the value is more, the surplus is a trust; if less, he recovers the deficiency... The pledgee may demand his debt and imprison the pledgor for it... The pledgee is ordered to present the pledge first...
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the Hanafi ruling on the pledgee's (murtahin) liability after taking possession (qabd). The text establishes that the pledge becomes 'madhmun' – a guarantee – meaning the pledgee is liable for the lesser of the pledge's value and the debt. This contrasts with al-Shafi'i, who treats the pledge as an amanah (trust) with no liability, and Malik, who distinguishes between apparent and hidden causes of destruction. The Hanafi proof includes a hadith where the Prophet ruled that a horse died and the pledgee's right was gone, indicating loss offsets the debt. The commentary further explains the valuation: the value is assessed at the time of possession. If the pledge is destroyed, three scenarios apply: (1) value equals debt – pledgee is deemed to have received full payment; (2) value exceeds debt – pledgee receives debt amount, surplus is trust in his hands; (3) value less than debt – pledgee receives that amount, balance remains due. Additionally, the pledgee has the right to demand the debt and even seek imprisonment of the pledgor, even while holding the pledge. The procedure requires the pledgee to present the pledge first to verify its existence, then the pledgor must pay the debt. If the pledge is in another city without transport costs, the same rule applies; otherwise, the pledgee may collect his debt without bringing the pledge.

Key Terms
مضمون (madhmūn): Guaranteed; meaning the pledgee becomes a guarantor for the pledged item up to the lesser of its value or the debt.
أمانة (amānah): Trust; the pledgor's ownership remains, and the pledge is a trust in the pledgee's hands such that he is not liable for loss unless negligent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man pledges a cow worth 1000 dirhams to secure a debt of 800 dirhams. The cow dies accidentally in the pledgee's possession. According to the Hanafi school, what is the outcome?
A) The pledgee must pay 1000 dirhams to the pledgor.
B) The debt of 800 dirhams is considered paid, and the pledgee owes nothing.
C) The debt is reduced by 800 dirhams, and the surplus 200 is lost by the pledgor.
D) The debt remains fully due, and the pledgee is not liable.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling limits the pledgee's liability to the lesser of the pledge's value and the debt, with three clear scenarios for destruction.

Pledge Valuation: The Hanafi Rule of Securing the Lesser Value
Ruling on pledge valuation and effects of destruction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that a pledge secures the lesser of its value and the debt, and apply it to three destruction scenarios (value equal to debt, greater than debt, less than debt).

Core Idea
A pledge secures only the lesser of its value and the debt; surplus is a trust.

This passage from a Hanafi fiqh text explains the legal effect when a pledged item is destroyed. The key principle is that the pledge is security for the lesser amount — either its own value or the debt it secures. Understanding this rule avoids unfair enrichment and ensures proper settlement. We will unpack the three possible outcomes and the evidentiary rule if the parties disagree on value.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core principle
Read the opening statement: the pledge is secured by the lesser of its value and the debt. This is the foundation for all subsequent rulings.
2
Analyze the three destruction scenarios
Work through each: (a) value equals debt → debt satisfied; (b) value exceeds debt → debt satisfied, surplus is a trust; (c) value less than debt → debt partially satisfied, creditor can claim remainder.
Note that the surplus in (b) is a trust (amana), not owned by the pledgee.
3
Determine the valuation date
The passage specifies value is assessed at the time of possession because the pledge enters the pledgee's liability from that moment.
4
Resolve a valuation dispute
If parties disagree on value, the pledgee's word is taken because he denies a higher value; the pledgor can provide evidence to prove a higher value.
This aligns with the principle that the burden of proof is on the claimant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحكام الرهن بأقل من قيمته ومن الدين، فلو هلك وقيمته مثل دينه صار مستوفيا دينه، وإن كانت أكثر من دينه فالفضل أمانة، وبقدر الدين صار مستوفيا، وإن كانت أقل صار مستوفيا بقدره، ورجع الراهن بالفضل، وتعتبر القيمة يوم القبض؛ لأنه يومئذ دخل في ضمانه، وإن اختلفا في القيمة، فالقول للمرتهن؛ لأنه ينكر الزيادة، والبينة للراهن؛ لأنه يثبتها.
Rulings of the pledge: it is secured by the lesser of its value and the debt. If it perishes and its value equals the debt, the pledgee becomes paid his debt. If it is more than the debt, the surplus is a trust and he becomes paid to the extent of the debt. If it is less, he becomes paid to its extent and the pledgor is sought for the remainder. Value is considered on the day of possession because on that day it entered his liability. If they differ about the value, the word is of the pledgee because he denies the increase; the evidence is on the pledgor because he claims it.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage states the ruling on how a pledge (rahn) is secured. The Hanafi position: the pledge is security for the lesser of its value and the debt. The commentator explains that if the pledge perishes in the pledgee's custody without his negligence, the outcome depends on its value relative to the debt. If equal, the debt is considered fully satisfied. If the pledge is worth more, the debtor is considered paid to the amount of the debt and the surplus is a trust in the pledgee's hands; if he misuses it he becomes liable. If the pledge is worth less, the debtor is paid only to that extent and the creditor can claim the remaining balance from the debtor. The value is assessed at the time of possession because the pledge enters the pledgee's liability at that moment. If the two parties disagree on the value, the pledgee's statement is taken because he denies the excess, while the burden of proof is on the pledgor to establish the higher value.

Key Terms
الرهن (al-rahn): A contract where property is held as security for a debt; the pledgee has the right to retain it until payment.
المرتهن (al-murtahin): The creditor who receives the pledge as security.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a pledge worth 100 dirhams secures a debt of 80 dirhams, and the pledge perishes without the pledgee's fault, what happens?
A) The debt is fully cancelled, and the pledgee keeps the pledge.
B) The debt is satisfied to the extent of 80 dirhams, and the surplus of 20 dirhams is a trust in the pledgee's hands.
C) The debt remains, and the pledgee must compensate the pledgor for the loss.
D) The pledgee receives the full 80 dirhams and the pledgor gets nothing.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule: a pledge secures only the lesser of its value and the debt; any surplus is a trust, and the pledgee's word on value is accepted unless the pledgor provides evidence.

Pledgee's Right to Demand Debt and Imprison
Ruling on pledgee's right to demand debt and imprison the pledgor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the pledgee's procedural rights regarding debt collection and identify when producing the pledge is or is not required.

Core Idea
Pledgee may demand debt and imprison pledgor, but must first produce the pledge; transport not required if burdensome.

In a pledge (rahn) contract, the pledgee has the right to seek repayment of the debt. However, this right is subject to procedural rules that balance the interests of both parties. This lesson clarifies when the pledgee can demand payment and even imprison the pledgor, and crucially, when he must bring the pledge to the pledgor's location.

Guided Path
1
Identify the pledgee's core rights
Read the passage and list the two main rights the pledgee has against the pledgor.
The rights are: demanding the debt and imprisoning for nonpayment.
2
Determine the procedural order
Before the pledgor can be forced to pay, what must the pledgee do first? This order is a condition for using coercion.
The pledgee must produce (bring) the pledge first.
3
Analyze the obligation to produce the pledge
If the pledgee demands payment in a different city, does he still have to bring the pledge? Distinguish between a pledge with no burden (light) and one with burden (heavy/costly).
If no burden, same rule; if burden, pledgee can collect without bringing the pledge.
4
Distinguish between immediate and deferred debt
When can the pledgee demand payment? Note the condition regarding the debt's maturity.
Demand is allowed only when the debt is due (immediate) or at term expiration for deferred.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وله أن يطالب الراهن بدينه، ويحبسه للمرتهن أي وقت شاء به، ويؤمر المرتهن بإحضار رهنه والراهن بأداء دينه أولا، وإن طالبه بالدين في غير البلد الذي وقع العقد فيه، فإن كان الرهن لا حمل له ولا مؤونة، فكذلك الجواب؛ لأن الأماكن كلها في حقه كبقعة واحدة في حق التسليم، وإن كان له حمل ومؤونة، فيستوفي دينه، ولا يكلف إحضار الرهن؛ لأن الواجب عليه التسليم بالتخلية دون النقل؛ لأنه يتضرر به زيادة ضرر ولم تلزمه في العقد، هذا إذا كان الدين حالا، أما إذا كان مؤجلا، فيطالبه عند حلول الأجل.
And he (the pledgee) has the right to demand his debt from the pledgor and to imprison him for it at any time he wishes. And the pledgee is ordered to bring his pledge first, and the pledgor to pay his debt first. If he demands the debt in a city other than where the contract took place, if the pledge has no burden or expense, the answer is the same because all places are like one spot regarding delivery. If it has burden and expense, he collects his debt and is not required to bring the pledge because the obligation on him is delivery by vacating, not by transport, since that would cause extra harm and was not stipulated in the contract. This is if the debt is due; if it is deferred, he demands when the term expires.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage outlines the pledgee's procedural rights. The pledgee may demand payment of the debt at any time and may even have the pledgor imprisoned for nonpayment. However, before the pledgor is forced to pay, the pledgee must first produce the pledge to show it is intact. Then the pledgor is ordered to pay. If the pledgee demands payment in a different city than where the contract was made: if the pledge is light and easy to transport, the same rules apply; if moving it is burdensome, the pledgee can collect the debt without bringing the pledge, as he is only required to make it available (takhliyah), not physically transport it. This applies to an immediate debt; for a deferred debt, the demand is only at maturity.

Key Terms
الحبس (al-habs): Imprisonment as a means to compel payment of debt, permissible in Hanafi law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the pledgee demands the debt in a city other than where the contract was made, and the pledge is heavy and costly to transport, what must the pledgee do?
A) He must bring the pledge to the pledgor's city.
B) He may collect the debt without bringing the pledge.
C) He cannot demand payment at all until he returns to the original city.
D) The debt becomes void.

Key Takeaway
The pledgee may demand payment and imprison, but must first produce the pledge unless transport is burdensome.

Pledgee's Right to Retain Pledge Until Full Payment
Ruling on pledgee's retention right and post-payment destruction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that the pledgee may retain the pledge until full payment and explain the legal effect of the pledge's destruction after payment but before delivery.

Core Idea
Payment alone does not end the pledge; it lasts until physical return or discharge.

This lesson clarifies a key fiqh rule in pledge (rahn): the pledgee has the right to keep the pledge until the entire debt is paid. Many assume that paying the debt immediately dissolves the pledge, but the Hanafi school holds that the pledge remains in effect until it is physically returned to the pledgor or the pledgee explicitly discharges the debt. This has important practical consequences if the pledge is destroyed after payment but before return.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The passage states that the pledgee cannot be compelled to allow the sale of the pledge until the debt is fully paid. This establishes the pledgee's right to retain possession (al-habs) as security.
The pledgee is not forced to sell.
2
Determine the condition for return
Once the debt is fully paid, the pledgee must deliver the pledge to the pledgor. Payment is a necessary condition but not sufficient by itself — actual delivery or discharge is required.
Merely paying does not end the contract.
3
Analyze the destruction scenario
If the pledge perishes after payment but before delivery, the payment is considered void because the pledgee is deemed to have been satisfied from the time of initial possession (the pledge was security from the start). The pledgor recovers what he paid.
The second payment is redundant.
4
Understand the rationale
The text explains that the pledge contract persists until return or discharge. Therefore, the pledge remains in the pledgee's liability (damān) even after payment. Destruction after payment means the pledgee already had satisfaction, so the subsequent payment must be refunded.
The pledge is not dissolved by payment alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يمكنه من البيع حتى يقضيه الدين، فإذا قضى سلم الرهن، ولو هلك الرهن بعد قضاء الدين قبل تسليمه إلى الراهن استرد الراهن ما قضاه من الدين؛ لأنه تبين بالهلاك أنه صار مستوفيا من وقت القبض السابق، فكان الثاني استيفاء بعد استيفاء، فيجب رده، وهذا لأنه بإيفاء الدين لا ينفسخ الرهن حتى يرده إلى صاحبه فيكون مضمونا على حاله بعد قضاء الدين ما لم يسلمه إلى الراهن أو يبرئه المرتهن عن الدين.
He (the pledgee) cannot be made to allow the sale until the debtor pays the debt. When he pays, he delivers the pledge. If the pledge perishes after payment of the debt but before delivery to the pledgor, the pledgor recovers what he paid because it becomes clear that the pledgee was considered paid from the earlier possession, so the second payment was payment after payment, thus it must be returned. This is because by paying the debt the pledge is not dissolved until it is returned to its owner; thus it remains in his liability after payment until he delivers it to the pledgor or the pledgee discharges him from the debt.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage clarifies the pledgee's right of retention. The pledgee has the right to keep the pledge until the entire debt is paid; he cannot be forced to allow the pledgor to sell the pledge unless the debt is paid in full. Once the debt is fully paid, the pledge must be returned to the pledgor. If the pledge is destroyed after payment but before delivery, the payment is considered void because the pledgee had already received satisfaction from the earlier possession (the pledge was security from the start). Hence the pledgor gets his money back. The pledge contract remains in effect until actual return or the pledgee's discharge.

Key Terms
الحبس (al-habs): The right to retain the pledge until payment.
استيفاء (istifā'): Satisfaction; receipt of payment or fulfillment of the debt.
انفساخ (infisākh): Dissolution; the termination of the pledge contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The payment is lost and the pledgor receives nothing.
B) The pledgor can recover the payment because the pledgee is deemed satisfied from the earlier possession.
C) The pledgee must pay the value of the pledge.
D) The debt is considered unpaid and the pledgee keeps the pledge.

Key Takeaway
The pledge contract is not dissolved by mere payment; the pledge remains in the pledgee's custody and liability until actual return or discharge, and destruction after payment entitles the pledgor to reclaim the payment.

Pledgee's Use of the Pledge: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Ruling on pledgee's use of pledge and keeping with household

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the pledgee's use of the pledge and explain the Shafi'i counter-position, identifying the practical consequence of the difference.

Core Idea
No use of the pledge without permission — consent is the key.

The pledge (rahn) secures a debt, but who can use it? The Hanafi school strictly prohibits the pledgee from benefiting from the pledge without the pledgor's explicit permission. The Shafi'i school takes a more permissive stance, especially regarding the pledgor's use. This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq lays out the Hanafi position and highlights the disagreement, with an exception for certain intimate uses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what the text rules on the pledgee using the pledge. The answer: it is prohibited (haram) without permission.
2
List the types of use mentioned
The text specifies: employment (istikhdam), dwelling (sukna), wearing (lubs), leasing (ijara), and lending (i'ara). Recognise these as forms of intifa'.
The list is illustrative, not exhaustive.
3
Identify the condition for permissibility
The key condition is the pledgor's permission (idhn al-rahin). Without it, any use is prohibited.
4
Note the custody rule
The pledge may be kept with the pledgee himself, his wife, and his minor children in his household. This is custody, not use.
Custody is allowed; use requires permission.
5
Identify the Shafi'i disagreement and the exception
The text states 'contrary to al-Shafi'i' regarding the pledgor's use. The Hanafi exception for intercourse with a slave-girl and wearing a garment is mentioned, but Hanafi requires permission even for these.
The exception is part of the Shafi'i view; Hanafi holds that even these require permission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا ينتفع المرتهن بالرهن استخداما، وسكنى ولبسا وإجارة وإعارة إلا بإذن الراهن، ويحفظه بنفسه وزوجته وولده الذي في عياله، وكذا الراهن لا ينتفع إلا بإذن المرتهن، خلافا للشافعي إلا في وطء الجارية، ولبس الثوب.
And the pledgee does not benefit from the pledge by use, dwelling, wearing, leasing, or lending except with the pledgor's permission. And he keeps it with himself, his wife, and his child who are in his household. Likewise, the pledgor does not benefit except with the pledgee's permission, contrary to al-Shafi'i except in the case of intercourse with a slave-girl and wearing a garment.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage addresses the use of the pledged property. The Hanafi position: the pledgee cannot make any use of the pledge—such as employing a slave for service, living in a house, wearing clothes, renting it out, or lending it—without the pledgor's express permission. If he does so without permission, he is considered a transgressor (mut'addi) and becomes liable for any loss. However, he may keep the pledge in his own custody, and it may be kept by his wife, children, and servants who are in his household, as they are considered extensions of himself in terms of custody. Similarly, the pledgor also cannot use the pledge without the pledgee's permission. This differs from the Shafi'i school, which permits the pledgor to use the pledge in certain cases. The Hanafi exception: the pledgor may have intercourse with a female slave and may wear garments (since these uses do not diminish the property in a significant way? Actually the text says 'except' meaning the Shafi'i opinion allows, but the Hanafi position is that even these require permission).

Key Terms
المرتهن (al-murtahin): The pledgee (creditor) who holds the pledge as security.
الراهن (al-rahin): The pledgor (debtor) who gives the pledge as security.
الرهن (al-rahn): The pledged property or collateral.
الانتفاع (al-intifa'): Using the pledge for personal benefit, such as service, dwelling, wearing, etc.
الإذن (al-idhn): Permission from the owner or holder of the right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a pledgee wears a garment given as a pledge without the pledgor's permission, what is the ruling?
A) It is permissible because it does not damage the garment.
B) It is not permissible, and the pledgee becomes liable if it is damaged.
C) It is permissible only if the pledgee pays rent.
D) It is permissible if the debt is small.

Key Takeaway
The pledgee may NOT benefit from the pledge without the pledgor's permission; any unauthorized use makes him liable for loss.

Creditor's Right to Retain Pledged Property Until Full Repayment
Ruling on creditor's retention right and post-repayment loss

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the creditor's right of retention in a pledge contract and apply the rule of post-payment loss.

Core Idea
The creditor may retain the pledge until full payment; even partial payment does not release it.

This lesson explores the creditor's right to retain pledged property as security. The Hanafi school emphasizes that retention is a fundamental incident of the pledge contract, protecting the creditor's interest until the debt is fully satisfied. We will examine the rules of release and the effect of loss after payment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
State the creditor's right: he may retain the pledged property until the debt is fully paid. The debtor cannot force him to sell the pledge.
2
Understand the scope of retention
The creditor's obligation is only to vacate possession (takhliya), not to transfer or allow sale. He keeps the pledge by himself, his wife, or household members.
3
Analyze the effect of partial payment
If the debtor pays only part of the debt, the creditor may still retain the entire pledge until the remainder is paid, analogous to retention in a sale contract.
This is supported by qiyas to bay'.
4
Examine post-payment loss
If the pledge is destroyed after full payment but before delivery, the debtor recovers his payment; the pledge remains under the creditor's liability until actual return.
The contract is not dissolved until delivery or release.
5
Consider dissolution of the contract
Even if the pledge contract is dissolved, the creditor may still retain the pledge until the debt is paid, and destruction remains against the debt.
This underscores the security nature of rahn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحكام الرهن في يد المرتمن لا يمكنه من البيع حتى يقضيه الدين، فإذا قضى سلم الرهن، ولا ينتفع الراهن المرتمن [؟] الراهن جميع الدين المرتمن إلا بإذن الراهن المرقمن بالرهن استخداما، وسكنى ولبسا وإجارة وإعارة ويحفظه بنفسه وزوجته وولده الذي في عياله الرهن؛ لأن الواجب عليه التسليم بالتخلية دون النقل؛ لأنه يتضرر به زيادة ضرر و لم تلزمه في العقد، هذا إذا كان الدين حالا، أما إذا كان مؤجلا ، فيطالبه عند حلول الأجل. تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٧٩/٨] (ملا مسكين) لا يمكنه من البيع إلخ أي لو أراد الراهن أن يبيع الرهن لا يجب على المرتمن أن يمكنه من البيع حتى يقضي الراهن الدين؛ لأن حكم الرهن الحبس إلى أن يقضي الدين، فلو قضاه البعض، فله أن يحبس كل الرهن حتى يستوفي البقية كما في حبس المبيع. [رمز الحقائق: ٤٠٦/٢] فإذا قضى إلخ أي إذا قضى الراهن جميع الدين سلم المرتمن الرهن إليه؛ لزوال المانع من التسليم؛ لوصول حق المرتمن إليه، فلو هلك الرهن بعد قضاء الدين قبل تسليمه إلى الراهن استرد الراهن ما قضاه من الدين؛ لأنه تبين بالهلاك أنه صار مستوفيا من وقت القبض السابق، فكان الثاني استيفاء بعد استيفاء، فيجب رده، وهذا لأنه بإيفاء الدين لا ينفسخ الرهن حتى يرده إلى صاحبه فيكون مضمونا على حاله بعد قضاء الدين ما لم يسلمه إلى الراهن أو يبرئه المرتمن عن الدين، وكذا لو فسخا الرهن لا ينفسخ ما دام في يده حتى كان للمرتهن أن يمنعه بعد الفسخ حتى يستوفي دينه، ولو هلك بعد الفسخ يكون كما لو هلك قبله فيكون هالكا بدينه.
The rulings of pledge: In the hand of the creditor, he cannot allow [the debtor] to sell until he pays the debt. When he pays, he delivers the pledge. And the creditor [?] the debtor all debt the creditor except with permission of the debtor [?] and he keeps it by himself, his wife, and his child who are in his household, because the obligation upon him is delivery by vacating (takhliya) without transfer, because that would cause him excessive harm, and it was not incumbent upon him in the contract. This is if the debt is due; if it is deferred, he demands it at the term. [From Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 8/479] (Mulla Miskin) 'He cannot allow him to sell etc.' i.e., if the debtor wants to sell the pledge, the creditor is not obliged to allow him to sell until the debtor pays the debt, because the ruling of pledge is retention (habs) until the debt is paid. If he pays part, he may retain the entire pledge until he recovers the remainder, as in retention of the sold item. [Rumuz al-Haqa'iq: 2/406] 'When he pays etc.' i.e., when the debtor pays the entire debt, the creditor delivers the pledge to him, because the impediment to delivery is removed due to the creditor's right being fulfilled. If the pledge is destroyed after payment but before delivery to the debtor, the debtor recovers what he paid of the debt, because it becomes clear through destruction that he became a recipient from the time of the earlier taking, so the second was taking after taking, so it must be returned. This is because by paying the debt, the pledge is not dissolved until he returns it to its owner, so it remains under liability until delivered to the debtor or the creditor releases him from the debt. Similarly, if they dissolve the pledge contract, it is not dissolved as long as it is in his hand, so the creditor may prevent [delivery] after dissolution until he recovers his debt. If it is destroyed after dissolution, it is as if destroyed before, so it is destroyed against the debt.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage establishes the fundamental ruling of pledge (rahn): the creditor has the right to retain the pledged property until the debt is fully paid. The creditor is not obliged to allow the debtor to sell the pledge to satisfy the debt; he may wait for payment. The author explains that the creditor's obligation is to vacate the pledge (takhliya), meaning to hand over possession, but he is not required to transfer ownership or permit sale. If the debt is due, the creditor can demand payment; if deferred, he waits until the term. The commentary from Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies that partial payment does not require release of the pledge; the creditor may retain the entire pledge until the remainder is paid, analogous to the rule for a sold item. After full payment, the creditor must deliver the pledge. If the pledge is destroyed after payment but before delivery, the debtor recovers his payment because the pledge remained under the creditor's liability; the destruction shows that the payment was effectively not received. Similarly, if the parties cancel the pledge contract, the creditor still holds the pledge as security until the debt is paid; destruction after cancellation is treated as a loss against the debt.

Key Terms
المرتمن (al-murtamin): The creditor who receives the pledge (mortgagee).
الرهن (al-rahn): The property given as security for a debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The creditor's right of retention until full payment
B) The debtor's need for the property
C) The contract type being a sale
D) The timing of the debt

Key Takeaway
The pledged property remains under the creditor's control until full repayment, and even after payment, the creditor bears responsibility until delivery.

Prohibition of Creditor Using the Pledge Without Permission
Ruling on creditor's use of pledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for the creditor's use of a pledge and explain the liability consequences under Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
The pledge is for retention, not benefit; unauthorized use makes the creditor a transgressor but does not void the pledge.

In a rahn contract, the creditor holds the pledged property as security for the debt. This lesson clarifies that the creditor's right is limited to retention; any use of the property without the debtor's permission is prohibited and triggers liability implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal status of the pledge
The pledge is held for security (حبس) until debt recovery, not for benefit.
2
State the default prohibition
The creditor may not benefit from the pledge without the debtor's permission.
3
Analyze the effect of permission
If permitted, the pledge becomes a loan (ariyah) for liability purposes; destruction during use does not reduce the debt.
4
Distinguish between authorized and unauthorized use
Unauthorized use makes the creditor a transgressor with liability for usurpation (ghasb); the pledge contract remains valid.
5
Note that transgression does not invalidate the pledge
The pledge contract continues despite the creditor's transgression.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا ينتفع المرتمن إلخ لأن الرهن يقتضي الحبس إلى أن يستوفي دينه دون الانتفاع، فلا يجوز الانتفاع إلا بتسليط من الراهن، وإن فعل كان متعديا ولا يبطل الرهن بالتعدي، قال في "المبسوط": وليس للمرتهن أن ينتفع بالمرهون إلا بإذن الراهن، فإذا أذن له جاز أن يفعل ما أذن له فيه، ولو فعل غير ما أذن صار ضامنا، وإن ترك الاستعمال، عاد؛ لكونه ،رهنا ولو استعمل الرهن بإذن المرتمن، فإن هلك حالة الانتفاع لم يسقط من الدين شيء؛ لأنه بالإذن صار مقبوضا بحكم العارية، وإن خالف وهلك في حال الاستعمال ضمن ضمان الغصب. تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٨٠/٨] وكذا الراهن لا ينتفع إلا بإذن المرتمن، قال في "العزمية": أما كون حكم المرتمن ذلك، فمذكور في عامة المتون، وأما كون حكم الراهن ذلك، فمأخوذ من "المجمع" ونسبه في "غاية "البيان" إلى "الأقطع" حيث قال: قال أصحابنا: لا يجوز للراهن استيفاء منافع الرهن إلا بإذن المرتهن، وكذلك التصرف فيه خلافا للشافعي إلا في وطء الجارية، ولبس الثوب. (فتح الله المعين) استخداما : أي من حيث الاستخدام في الرقيق وسكنى: أي من حيث السكني إذا كان الرهن دارا. ولبسا : أي من حيث اللبس في الثياب (عيني)
The creditor may not benefit [from the pledge] because the pledge requires retention until he recovers his debt, not benefit. So benefit is not permitted except with permission from the debtor. If he does, he is a transgressor, but the pledge is not invalidated by transgression. He said in al-Mabsut: The creditor may not benefit from the pledge except with the debtor's permission. If he permits, he may do what is permitted; if he does something else, he becomes liable. If he leaves use, it reverts to being a pledge. If he uses with permission and it is destroyed during use, nothing is deducted from the debt because by permission it became taken as a loan (ariyah). If he violates and it is destroyed during use, he is liable as for usurpation. [Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 8/480] Similarly, the debtor may not benefit except with the creditor's permission. He said in al-Uzmiyyah: As for the ruling for the creditor, it is mentioned in all texts; as for the ruling for the debtor, it is taken from al-Majma' and attributed in Ghayat al-Bayan to al-Aqta' where he said: Our companions said: The debtor is not permitted to enjoy the benefits of the pledge except with the creditor's permission, and likewise disposition regarding it, contrary to al-Shafi'i except for intercourse with the slave-girl and wearing the garment. (Fath Allah al-Mu'in) 'Use' refers to employing slaves; 'residence' if the pledge is a house; 'wearing' for clothes.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage addresses the prohibition of using the pledged property without permission. The pledge is solely for security; the creditor may only retain it, not use it. Any use without the debtor's permission makes the creditor a transgressor, but the pledge contract remains valid. If the creditor uses with permission, it becomes a loan (ariyah); if destroyed, no deduction from the debt; if he exceeds permission, he is liable for usurpation. Similarly, the debtor cannot use the pledge without the creditor's permission, except for intercourse with a slave-girl or wearing a garment, according to a difference with al-Shafi'i who generally allows the debtor more freedom. The explanation of 'use' includes employing slaves, residing in a house, and wearing clothes.

Key Terms
المرتمن (al-murtamin): The creditor (pledgee) who holds the pledge.
الراهن (al-rahin): The debtor who gives the pledge.
العارية (al-ariyah): A loan of property for use without compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The creditor must compensate the debtor
B) Nothing is deducted from the debt
C) The creditor becomes a usurper
D) The pledge is invalidated

Key Takeaway
The creditor may not use the pledge without permission; if permitted, it is an ariyah; unauthorized use makes him liable but does not invalidate the pledge.

Prohibition of Debtor Using the Pledge Without Permission and the Shafi'i Dispute
Ruling on debtor's use of pledge and dispute with Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position prohibiting the debtor from using the pledge without permission and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
The debtor's ownership is restricted by the pledge; he cannot use the property without the creditor's permission, except for intercourse with a slave-girl and wearing a garment.

While the debtor owns the pledged property, the creditor's right to hold it as security limits the debtor's use. The Hanafi school generally prohibits the debtor from benefiting from the pledge without the creditor's permission, with two exceptions: intercourse with a slave-girl and wearing a garment. This lesson explores the reasoning and the disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
State the general Hanafi rule
The debtor may not use the pledge without the creditor's permission.
2
Identify the exceptions
Intercourse with a pledged slave-girl and wearing a pledged garment are permitted without explicit permission.
These are acknowledged in the text as exceptions.
3
Understand the basis
The pledge is for security; use diminishes value or rights, so permission is needed.
4
Present the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i generally allows the debtor to use the pledge as long as it does not impair the security.
5
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafi holds the debtor liable for damages from unauthorized use; Shafi'i considers it permissible without liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

If he permits, he may do what is permitted; if he does something else, he becomes liable. If he leaves use, it reverts to being a pledge. If he uses with permission and it is destroyed during use, nothing is deducted from the debt because by permission it became taken as a loan (ariyah). If he violates and it is destroyed during use, he is liable as for usurpation. [Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 8/480] Similarly, the debtor may not benefit except with the creditor's permission. He said in al-Uzmiyyah: As for the ruling for the creditor, it is mentioned in all texts; as for the ruling for the debtor, it is taken from al-Majma' and attributed in Ghayat al-Bayan to al-Aqta' where he said: Our companions said: The debtor is not permitted to enjoy the benefits of the pledge except with the creditor's permission, and likewise disposition regarding it, contrary to al-Shafi'i except for intercourse with the slave-girl and wearing the garment. (Fath Allah al-Mu'in) 'Use' refers to employing slaves; 'residence' if the pledge is a house; 'wearing' for clothes.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage addresses the prohibition of using the pledged property without permission. The pledge is solely for security; the creditor may only retain it, not use it. Any use without the debtor's permission makes the creditor a transgressor, but the pledge contract remains valid. If the creditor uses with permission, it becomes a loan (ariyah); if destroyed, no deduction from the debt; if he exceeds permission, he is liable for usurpation. Similarly, the debtor cannot use the pledge without the creditor's permission, except for intercourse with a slave-girl or wearing a garment, according to a difference with al-Shafi'i who generally allows the debtor more freedom. The explanation of 'use' includes employing slaves, residing in a house, and wearing clothes.

Key Terms
المرتمن (al-murtamin): The creditor (pledgee) who holds the pledge.
الراهن (al-rahin): The debtor who gives the pledge.
العارية (al-ariyah): A loan of property for use without compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is an exception that allows the debtor to use the pledge without the creditor's permission?
A) Sleeping in the pledged house
B) Wearing a pledged garment
C) Driving a pledged car
D) Eating from the pledged food

Key Takeaway
The debtor may not use the pledge without permission, except for intercourse with a slave-girl and wearing a garment; this is a Hanafi position disputed by al-Shafi'i.

Rahn — Pledge
intermediate
10 min
Custody and Expenses of the Pledged Property
Rulings on custody of the pledge, liability for misdeposit, and expenses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rules of custody for a pledge, identify when the creditor becomes liable for the entire value, and distinguish between expenses borne by the creditor and those borne by the debtor.

Core Idea
The creditor holds the pledge as a trust; preservation costs are on the creditor, maintenance costs on the debtor.

When a creditor takes possession of a pledge, he becomes a trustee (amin) over it. But what happens if he stores it elsewhere? Who pays for feed, guard, and land tax? The Hanafi school provides a detailed division based on the nature of the expense. This lesson unpacks the rules of custody, liability for transgression, and the allocation of costs between the debtor and creditor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default rule of custody
The creditor must keep the pledge himself or through his household members (wife, children, servants living with him). This is because he is a trustee, analogous to a depositee.
The pledge is an amanah (trust) like a deposit (wadi'ah).
2
Determine when the creditor becomes liable
If the creditor keeps the pledge with a stranger (outside his household) or deposits it elsewhere without necessity, or transgresses, he becomes liable for the entire value of the pledge, like a usurper.
Liability is strict for deviation from the allowed custodians.
3
Categorise the expenses
Identify each expense and classify it as either for preservation or for maintenance/return. Preservation costs (guard's wage, house rent, treating wounds) are on the creditor; maintenance costs (food, drink, shepherd, clothing, kharaj, 'ushr) are on the debtor.
The key principle: 'everything needed for preservation is on the creditor; everything needed for benefit and survival is on the debtor.'
4
Note the intra-Hanafi difference
Abu Yusuf holds that shelter rent (ajarat al-ma'wa) is on the debtor like other maintenance costs. The majority view is that it is on the creditor.
The mufta bihi is the majority view.
5
Apply the rule on taxes
Kharaj and 'ushr are on the debtor because they are attached to the land. However, if the government takes them from the creditor, he cannot recover the amount from the debtor.
The 'ushr takes precedence over the creditor's right in the pledged property.

Source Text

ويحفظه إلخ أي يحفظ المرتمن الرهن بنفسه وزوجته وولده الذي في عياله، وخادمه الذي في عياله؛ لأن عليه أمانة على ما بيناه فصار كالوديعة فقوله: "الذي في عياله - وخادمه الذي في عياله، وضمن بحفظه بغيرهم وبإيداعه وتعديه قيمته. وأجرة بيت حفظه وحافظه على المرتمن، وأجرة راعيه ونفقة الرهن والخراج على الراهن. (أي حفظ الرهن) الرهن إذا كان حيوانا والعشر متعلق بالأخيرين وأجيره الخاص وهو الذي استأجره مشاهرة أو مسانهة كولده الذي في عياله، والمعتبر فيه المساكنة، ولا عبرة بالنفقة حتى أن المرأة لو دفعته إلى زوجها لا تضمن كذا في "التكملة" و"الفتح". ضمن بحفظه إلخ [أي بحفظ الرهن أي ضمن المرتمن جميع قيمة الرهن لما بيناه أن عينه وديعة، والوديعة تضمن بهذه الأشياء؛ لكونه متعديا بها، فيضمن جميع قيمته كالمغصوب، ثم إن قضى القاضي بالقيمة من جنس الدين يلتقيان قصاصا بمجرد القضاء إذا كان الدين حالا، فلا يطالب كل واحد منهما صاحبه إلا بالفضل، وإن كان مؤجلا يضمن المرتمن قيمته ويكون رهنا عنده؛ لأنه بدل الرهن فيكون له حكم أصله، فإذا حل الأجل أخذه بدينه، وإن قضى بالقيمة من خلاف جنس الدين كان الضمان رهنا عنده إلى أن يقضيه دينه؛ لأنه بدل الرهن، فأخذ حكمه تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٨٢/٨] (فتح) وبإيداعه وكذا يضمن بإيداعه. (عيني) قیمته: منصوب؛ لأنه مفعول لقوله "ضمن". وأجرة بيت حفظه إلخ والأصل فيه: أن كل ما يحتاج إليه لمصلحة الرهن وتبقيته، فهو على الراهن سواء كان في الرهن فضل أو لا؛ لأن العين باقية على ملكه، وكذا منافعه مملوكة له. تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٨٣/٨] وذلك مثل النفقة من مأكله ومشربه وأجرة الراعي وكسوة الرقيق وأجرة ظئر ولد الرهن وكري النهر وسقي البستان وتلقيح نخلة وجذاذه والقيام بمصالحه، وكل ما كان لحفظه، أو لرده إلى يد الراهن، أو لرد جزء منه كمداواة الجرح، فهو على المرتهن مثل أجرة الحافظ وأجرة البيت الذي يحفظه فيه، وعن أبي يوسف: أن أجرة المأوى على الراهن بمنزلة النفقة، ومن هذا القسم جعل الآبق إذا كان كله مضمونا؛ لأن يد الاستيفاء كانت ثابتة على المحل ويحتاج إلى إعادة يد الاستيفاء ليرده على المالك، فكانت من مؤونة الرد، فتكون عليه، وإن كان بعضه أمانة، فيقدر المضمون على المرتمن وحصة الأمانة على الراهن رمز الحقائق: ٤٠٧/٢] على المرتمن إلخ فلو شرط المرتمن أن أجرة حفظه على الراهن لا يلزم الراهن منه شيء؛ لأن الحفظ واجب على المرتهن، بخلاف الوديعة؛ لأن الحفظ غير واجب على المودع. (رد المحتار بتغيير) والخراج الخ: أي خراج الأرض، وكذا العشر على الراهن، قال العيني: وأما العشر: فهو مقدم على حق المرتمن؛ لتعلقه بالعين، ولا يبطل الرهن به في الباقي؛ لأن وجوبه لا ينافي ملكه بخلاف استحقاق جزء شائع من حيث يبطل الرهن في الباقي؛ لأنه تبين بالاستحقاق أنه لا يملك قدر المستحق، فكان الرهن شائعا وتبين أن الرهن كان باطلا رمز الحقائق: ٤٠٧/٢] قال في "رد المحتار: وفي "البزازية": أخذ السلطان الخراج أو العشر من المرتمن لا يرجع على الراهن؛ لأنه إن تطوع فهو متبرع، وإن أكرهه فقد ظلمه السلطان، والمظلوم لا يرجع على الظالم. (رد المحتار)
The commentary: 'He keeps it etc.' i.e., the creditor keeps the pledge by himself, his wife, and his child who are in his household, and his servant who is in his household, because he has a trust as we have explained, so it is like a deposit (wadi'ah). So his statement: 'who are in his household' - and his servant who is in his household. And he is liable if he keeps it by others than these, and by depositing it with them, and by his transgression, for the entire value. And the rent of the house for keeping it and the guard are upon the creditor. And the rent of the shepherd and the sustenance of the pledge and the land tax are upon the debtor. (i.e., keeping the pledge) the pledge if it is an animal, and the 'ushr (tithe) is attached to the last two. And his special hired worker, who is hired monthly or yearly like his own child in his household, and the criterion is co-residence, not expense; even if a woman gives it to her husband, she is not liable. So in al-Takmila and al-Fath. 'He is liable by keeping it etc.' i.e., by keeping the pledge, the creditor becomes liable for the entire value of the pledge, because we have explained that the object is a deposit, and the deposit is liable by these things because he is a transgressor thereby, so he is liable for the entire value like a usurped object. Then if the judge gives judgment for the value from the same type of debt, they set off mutually by the judgment alone if the debt is due, so neither claims from the other except the surplus. If the debt is deferred, the creditor is liable for its value and it becomes a pledge with him, because it is a substitute for the pledge, so it has the ruling of the original. When the term arrives, he takes it for his debt. If the judge gives judgment for the value from a different type than the debt, the guarantee is a pledge with him until he pays his debt, because it is a substitute for the pledge, so it takes its ruling. [Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 8/482] (Fath) 'And by depositing it' also he is liable by depositing it. (Ayni) 'its value' is in the accusative because it is the object of 'he is liable'. 'And the rent of the house for keeping it etc.' The basis is that everything needed for the benefit and preservation of the pledge is upon the debtor, whether there is surplus in the pledge or not, because the property remains his ownership, and likewise its benefits are owned by him. [Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 8/483] Examples: sustenance of food and drink, rent of the shepherd, clothing of slaves, rent of the wet nurse for the pledge's baby, digging of the canal, watering the garden, pollination of the palm tree, harvesting of dates, and attending to its interests. And everything that is for its preservation, or for returning it to the debtor's hand, or for returning part of it like treating a wound, is upon the creditor, such as the guard's wage and the rent of the house in which he keeps it. According to Abu Yusuf, the rent of the shelter is upon the debtor like sustenance. From this category, the runaway slave if the entire [value] is guaranteed, because the hand of enforcement was established over the property and needs to re-establish the enforcing hand to return him to the owner, so it is part of the cost of return, so it is upon him. And if part of it is a trust, then the guaranteed portion is estimated as upon the creditor and the trust share upon the debtor. [Rumuz al-Haqa'iq: 2/407] 'upon the creditor etc.' So if the creditor stipulates that his preserve fee is upon the debtor, nothing is required from the debtor, because preservation is obligatory upon the creditor, unlike the deposit because preservation is not obligatory upon the depositee. (Radd al-Muhtar with modification) 'And the kharaj etc.' i.e., the land tax, and likewise the 'ushr (tithe) are upon the debtor. al-Ayni said: As for the 'ushr, it takes precedence over the creditor's right because it is attached to the property, and the pledge is not invalidated by it in the remainder, because its obligation does not contradict ownership, unlike the entitlement of a share in common, because then the pledge is invalid in the remainder, because it becomes clear through the entitlement that he does not own the amount entitled, so the pledge was common and it becomes clear that the pledge was void. [Rumuz al-Haqa'iq: 2/407] He said in Radd al-Muhtar: In al-Bazzaziyya: If the sultan takes the kharaj or 'ushr from the creditor, he does not have recourse against the debtor, because if he paid voluntarily he is a benefactor, and if he was forced, the sultan has oppressed him, and the oppressed has no recourse against the oppressor. (Radd al-Muhtar)
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage details the rules of custody and expenses. The creditor must keep the pledge himself or through his household members (wife, children, servants living with him). If he keeps it with others or deposits it elsewhere without reason, he becomes liable for the entire value. The expenses for preservation are divided: costs that maintain the pledge (food, drink, shepherd, etc.) are on the debtor because the property belongs to him; costs for guarding and storage (guard's wage, house rent) are on the creditor because he is the custodian. The creditor is liable for the full value if he transgresses, e.g., deposits it improperly. If the judge awards the value as a judgment, it may offset the debt if of the same type and due. The land tax (kharaj) and tithe ('ushr) are on the debtor. If the creditor pays them involuntarily, he cannot recover from the debtor.

Key Terms
الوديعة (al-wadi'ah): A deposit; the pledge is treated like a deposit for custody purposes.
الخراج (al-kharaj): Land tax on agricultural land.
العشر (al-'ushr): Tithe on agricultural produce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The debtor
B) The creditor
C) Shared equally
D) The judge decides

Key Takeaway
The pledge is a trust; the creditor is liable for transgression, and expenses are divided between creditor (preservation) and debtor (maintenance).

Expenses and Liability in the Pledge Contract (Rahn)
Explains who bears expenses of pledged property and mortgagee's liability for transgression.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between custody expenses (on the mortgagee) and maintenance expenses (on the mortgagor) in a pledge contract, and explain the mortgagee's liability for misappropriation (ta'addi).

Core Idea
The mortgagee bears custody costs and liability for transgression; the mortgagor bears maintenance and taxes.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq clarifies a common source of confusion in pledge (rahn): who pays for what? The answer hinges on the nature of the contract—the pledged property remains the mortgagor's ownership but the mortgagee is entrusted with custody. This lesson will unpack the allocation of expenses and the critical trigger that shifts liability onto the mortgagee.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their legal roles
Recall that in rahn, the mortgagor (rahin) retains ownership, while the mortgagee (murtahin) holds the property as a trust (amanah). This distinction is the foundation for all subsequent rulings.
The pledge is legally a trust (wadi'ah) in the mortgagee's hands.
2
Separate custody expenses from maintenance expenses
The passage lists expenses: guard's wage and house rent for custody are on the mortgagee; food, fodder, shepherd's wage, land tax, and 'ushr are on the mortgagor. Understand the principle: everything for preservation/benefit of the property is on the owner (mortgagor); everything for custody/return is on the custodian (mortgagee).
Abu Yusuf dissents on shelter rent, but the mufta bihi is the majority view.
3
Identify the mortgagee's liability trigger (ta'addi)
The mortgagee is not liable for loss or damage unless he transgresses (ta'addi). Ta'addi includes entrusting the pledge to a stranger, depositing it improperly, or using it without permission. Once he transgresses, he becomes liable for the full value.
Ta'addi removes the trust status, making the mortgagee like a usurper (ghasib).
4
Apply the qiyas (analogy) to ghasb
The text reasons: since the pledge is a trust, ta'addi triggers full liability by analogy to usurpation (ghasb). In ghasb, the wrongdoer is liable for the full value; similarly, the transgressing mortgagee must pay the full value of the pledge.
This is qiyas jaliy (clear analogy) with the illa of transgression.
5
Note the intra-madhhab dispute on shelter rent
Abu Yusuf holds that shelter rent (ajr al-ma'wa) is on the mortgagor, akin to maintenance. But the relied-upon position (zahir al-riwayah) places it on the mortgagee. Understand how different reasoning leads to different allocations.
The dispute does not affect the core principle; only the classification of shelter rent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحكام الرهن كتاب الرهن وخادمه الذي في عياله، وضمن بحفظه بغيرهم وبإيداعه وتعديه قيمته. وأجرة بيت أي المرتمن أي غير المذكورين المرتمن على الرهن حفظه وحافظه على المرتمن، وأجرة راعيه ونفقة الرهن والخراج على الراهن. أي حفظ الرهن الرهن إذا كان حيوانا والعشر متعلق بالأخيرين وأجيره الخاص وهو الذي استأجره مشاهرة أو مسانهة كولده الذي في عياله، والمعتبر فيه المساكنة، ولا عبرة بالنفقة حتى أن المرأة لو دفعته إلى زوجها لا تضمن كذا في "التكملة" و"الفتح". ضمن بحفظه إلخ [أي بحفظ الرهن أي ضمن المرتمن جميع قيمة الرهن لما بيناه أن عينه وديعة، والوديعة تضمن بهذه الأشياء؛ لكونه متعديا بها، فيضمن جميع قيمته كالمغصوب، ثم إن قضى القاضي بالقيمة من جنس الدين يلتقيان قصاصا بمجرد القضاء إذا كان الدين حالا، فلا يطالب كل واحد منهما صاحبه إلا بالفضل، وإن كان مؤجلا يضمن المرتمن قيمته ويكون رهنا عنده؛ لأنه بدل الرهن فيكون له حكم أصله، فإذا حل الأجل أخذه بدينه، وإن قضى بالقيمة من خلاف جنس الدين كان الضمان رهنا عنده إلى أن يقضيه دينه؛ لأنه بدل الرهن، فأخذ حكمه تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٨٢/٨] (فتح) وبإيداعه وكذا يضمن بإيداعه. (عيني) قیمته: منصوب؛ لأنه مفعول لقوله "ضمن". وأجرة بيت حفظه إلخ والأصل فيه: أن كل ما يحتاج إليه لمصلحة الرهن وتبقيته، فهو على الراهن سواء كان في الرهن فضل أو لا؛ لأن العين باقية على ملكه، وكذا منافعه مملوكة له. تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤٨٣/٨] وذلك مثل النفقة من مأكله ومشربه وأجرة الراعي وكسوة الرقيق وأجرة ظئر ولد الرهن وكري النهر وسقي البستان وتلقيح نخلة وجذاذه والقيام بمصالحه، وكل ما كان لحفظه، أو لرده إلى يد الراهن، أو لرد جزء منه كمداواة الجرح، فهو على المرتهن مثل أجرة الحافظ وأجرة البيت الذي يحفظه فيه، وعن أبي يوسف: أن أجرة المأوى على الراهن بمنزلة النفقة، ومن هذا القسم جعل الآبق إذا كان كله مضمونا؛ لأن يد الاستيفاء كانت ثابتة على المحل ويحتاج إلى إعادة يد الاستيفاء ليرده على المالك، فكانت من مؤونة الرد، فتكون عليه، وإن كان بعضه أمانة، فيقدر المضمون على المرتمن وحصة الأمانة على الراهن رمز الحقائق: ٤٠٧/٢] على المرتمن إلخ فلو شرط المرتمن أن أجرة حفظه على الراهن لا يلزم الراهن منه شيء؛ لأن الحفظ واجب على المرتهن، بخلاف الوديعة؛ لأن الحفظ غير واجب على المودع. (رد المحتار بتغيير) والخراج الخ: أي خراج الأرض، وكذا العشر على الراهن، قال العيني: وأما العشر: فهو مقدم على حق المرتمن؛ لتعلقه بالعين، ولا يبطل الرهن به في الباقي؛ لأن وجوبه لا ينافي ملكه بخلاف استحقاق جزء شائع من حيث يبطل الرهن في الباقي؛ لأنه تبين بالاستحقاق أنه لا يملك قدر المستحق، فكان الرهن شائعا وتبين أن الرهن كان باطلا رمز الحقائق: ٤٠٧/٢] قال في "رد المحتار: وفي "البزازية": أخذ السلطان الخراج أو العشر من المرتمن لا يرجع على الراهن؛ لأنه إن تطوع فهو متبرع، وإن أكرهه فقد ظلمه السلطان، والمظلوم لا يرجع على الظالم. (رد المحتار)
Rulings on the pledge. The book of pledge and its servant who is in his household. He is liable by guarding it by others, by depositing it, and by transgressing it—its value. The rent of the house (i.e., the mortgagee's house) is on the mortgagee; the guard and the guard's wage are on the mortgagee; the rent of the shepherd, the maintenance of the pledge, and the land tax are on the mortgagor. The 'ushr is also on the mortgagor. If the pledge is an animal, its fodder and the wages of its shepherd are on the mortgagor. The special hireling (hired monthly or yearly) is like his child in his household; consideration is given to cohabitation, not to expenditure, so if a woman gives it to her husband, she is not liable. He is liable by guarding it, etc. [i.e., by guarding the pledge, the mortgagee is liable for the full value because we have explained that the pledged property is a trust, and a trust becomes liable by these things due to transgression; so he is liable for the full value like a usurper. Then if the judge decrees the value from the same genus as the debt, they cancel each other by the mere decree if the debt is due; then neither party demands from the other except the surplus. If the debt is deferred, the mortgagee is liable for its value and it becomes a pledge with him, because it is a substitute for the pledge, so it takes the ruling of the original; when the term ends, he takes it for his debt. If the judge decrees the value from a different genus, the guarantee is a pledge with him until he pays his debt.] (Fath) By depositing it: likewise he is liable by depositing it. ('Ayni) Its value: accusative because it is the object of "he is liable". The rent of the house for guarding it, etc. The principle: everything needed for the benefit and preservation of the pledge is on the mortgagor, whether the pledge has surplus or not, because the property remains his ownership, and its benefits are owned by him. This includes maintenance like food and drink, wages of the shepherd, clothing of the slave, wages of the wet-nurse for the pledge's child, digging of the canal, watering of the orchard, pollination of the date-palm, harvesting, and management of its interests. Everything for its custody or to return it to the mortgagor's hand, or to return part of it like treatment of a wound, is on the mortgagee, such as the wage of the guard and the rent of the house where it is kept. From Abu Yusuf: the rent of the shelter is on the mortgagor like maintenance. From this category, if the slave absconds and is entirely guaranteed, because the hand of use was established over the property and needs to be re-established to return to the owner, so it is for the expense of return, so it is on him. If some of it is a trust, the guaranteed portion is apportioned to the mortgagee and the trust portion to the mortgagor. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq) On the mortgagee, etc. If the mortgagee stipulates that the wage for guarding is on the mortgagor, nothing is binding on the mortgagor, because guarding is obligatory on the mortgagee, unlike a deposit, where guarding is not obligatory on the depositee. (Radd al-Muhtar with modification) And the land tax: i.e., the land tax, and likewise the 'ushr is on the mortgagor. Al-'Ayni said: The 'ushr takes precedence over the mortgagee's right because it pertains to the property itself, and the pledge is not invalidated in the remainder because its obligation does not conflict with ownership, unlike the entitlement to a part of the property, which invalidates the pledge in the remainder because it becomes clear that he does not own the amount of the entitlement, so the pledge becomes shared and it becomes clear that the pledge was void. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq) He said in Radd al-Muhtar: In al-Bazzaziyya: If the sultan takes the land tax or 'ushr from the mortgagee, he cannot recover it from the mortgagor, because if he paid voluntarily he is a donor, and if forced, the sultan has wronged him, and the wronged person does not recover from the wrongdoer. (Radd al-Muhtar)
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the rulings (Ahkam) of pledge (rahn). It addresses two main issues: (1) the liability of the mortgagee (murtahin) for the pledge if he misappropriates it or entrusts it to others, and (2) who bears the expenses of the pledged property. The core ruling is that the mortgagee is liable for the full value of the pledge if he transgresses (ta'addi) by entrusting it to an unauthorized person or by depositing it improperly, because the pledge is considered a trust (wadi'ah) in his hands. Once he transgresses, he becomes like a usurper (ghasib) and is liable for the entire value. If the judge decrees a value equivalent to the debt, the two offset immediately if the debt is due; if the debt is deferred, the guaranteed value becomes a new pledge. Regarding expenses, there is a clear distinction: everything needed for the preservation and benefit of the pledge (maintenance, fodder, rent of pasture, medical treatment for wounds) is on the mortgagor (rahin) because the property remains his. However, expenses for custody (like the guard's wage and rent of the storeroom) are on the mortgagee because he is obligated to guard it. The text reports an intra-madhhab dispute: Abu Yusuf holds that the rent of the shelter is on the mortgagor like maintenance, but the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that it is on the mortgagee. Finally, the passage clarifies that land tax (kharaj) and tithe ('ushr) are on the mortgagor, and if the government collects them from the mortgagee, he cannot recover from the mortgagor. The reasoning is that the pledge is a trust; ta'addi triggers full liability via qiyas to ghasb.

Key Terms
الرهن (al-rahn): The contract of pledge or the pledged property itself; in this passage, it refers to the pledged object.
المرتمن (al-murtahin): The mortgagee (creditor) who receives the pledge as security.
الراهن (al-rahin): The mortgagor (debtor) who gives the pledge.
الوديعة (al-wadi'ah): A trust deposit; the pledge is legally analogous to a trust in terms of liability before transgression.
التعدي (al-ta'addi): Misappropriation or transgression; any act that removes the property from the trust status, such as entrusting to a stranger or depositing improperly.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as explained in this passage, who bears the expense of the guard who watches the pledged property?
A) The mortgagor (rahin) because the property is his.
B) The mortgagee (murtahin) because he is required to guard it.
C) Both equally.
D) The court (qadi) from the public treasury.

Key Takeaway
The allocation of expenses in a pledge hinges on the distinction between ownership (mortgagor bears maintenance and taxes) and custody (mortgagee bears custody costs and liability for transgression).

Pledging an Undivided Share (Musha') in Rahn
Inter-madhhab dispute with expansion on musha' and attached fruits

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on pledging an undivided share (musha'), explain the basis of continuous detention (habs da'im), distinguish the Shafi'i position, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
You cannot pledge what you cannot exclusively detain: continuous possession is the essence of rahn.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq tackles a vital question: can you pledge property you co-own? The Hanafi school answers with a clear 'no', and its reasoning revolves around the core requirement of rahn – continuous detention (habs da'im). The Shafi'i school disagrees, arguing that sale is the primary effect. This dispute reveals how each school defines the very purpose of the pledge contract. We will also examine the intra-Hanafi distinction between fasid (defective) and batil (void) pledges.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: pledging a musha' (shared property) is not valid. Note that the contract is fasid (defective), not batil.
The text says 'لا يصح رهن المشاع' – it is not valid.
2
Identify the opposing (Shafi'i) view and its basis
According to the passage, Imam al-Shafi'i allows pledging a musha' because the effect of the pledge is the right to sell upon default, and a share can be sold.
The Shafi'i focus is on the secondary effect (sale), not on detention.
3
Trace the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi proof is rational (dalil aqli): the primary effect of a pledge is continuous detention (habs da'im). In a musha', one cannot detain the property exclusively without a time-share (muhaya'ah), which is legally impermissible. This makes detention inconceivable.
The text says 'ولا يتصور الحبس الدائم في المشاع' – continuous detention is inconceivable in a musha'.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response to Shafi'i
The Hanafi school responds that detention is the primary purpose; the right to sell is secondary. If detention is impossible, the contract cannot be valid.
This is contrasted with gifts (hiba) and leases (ijara) where musha' is sometimes allowed because their primary effects (ownership, usufruct) do not require exclusive possession.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
If a musha' is pledged and possession is taken, according to Hanafis the contract is fasid, so the mortgagee is liable (daman). According to Shafi'is it is valid and no liability arises from possession.
Also note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: if shuyu' arises later, Abu Yusuf says it remains valid; Muhammad and the majority say it becomes invalid.
